# 277\n
{London, British Library, Cotton Caligula A.ix, part 1, hand A, fols. 3r-17rb (foot); 17va line 5-18vb line 6; 27ra lines 1-6 (BLIdE); 88ra-89rb line 3: Layamon A}\n

{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{~f3ra~}\n
{=I have tried to indicate capital letters as they appear in the MS which\n
are not necessarily the same as in the editions of Madden or of Brook\n
and Leslie (B&L). Unfortunately the rubrication used to indicate\n
capitals in the MS is not clear on black and white microfilm and\n
I have tended to rely more on majuscule shape to assign the * indicating\n
capitalisation and do not mention colour. There is bound to be some\n
inconsistency, however, in the final result. The line-breaks are those\n
of the MS which is not written in verse lines but as prose in two\n
columns. Rhyme is irregular and when it does occur is so often mixed\n
with assonance or half-rhyme that I have made no attempt to mark it\n
except where there is a clear (pure-rhyme) rhyming couplet marking the\n
end of a section of the story or sense unit. Note that in the\n
abbreviation for "that" - barred thorn - the bar touches the ascender of\n
thorn but does not bisect it. This figura does not seem to be used,\n
however, for an abbreviation for "there" and I have therefore realised\n
it as y~ not as yer. Contrast the hand of part 2 of this MS - Owl and\n
Nightingale - where the same figura, with the hook not touching the\n
ascender, is used for "that" and "there" and both have therefore been\n
realised as yer. Cf. also hand B of Layamon A similar to this. In fact\n
the main differences between hand A and hand B of Layamon are in the\n
generally greater regularity of hand A. It has both double and single\n
compartment A but seems never to have single compartment A with hooked\n
second element as is common in hand B. Hand B, however, is variable to\n
such an extent that most of hand A\'s characteristics are present in\n
his hand which makes separation of the stints a bit problematic=}\n
{>)*ROBERTUS *COTTON *BRUCE)>}\n
{=ins, in top margin in a modern hand=}\n
{(INCIPIT \'HYSTORIA BRUTONUM\'(}\n

$loth/ajpl_LAd+E $/plaj_+E\n
$fiend/npl_F[E]OnD $/pln_0\n
{=MS reading looks like FEOOnD - so reads Madden - but originally the\n
scribe wrote FREOnD, realised his mistake and overwrote the R with an\n
E failing to erase the original E which Madden has taken for O. Very\n
often the scribe\'s EO has an element of biting and resembles OO because\n
the lack of the usual space between the two letters gives a right side\n
closure to the E and if the hairstroke forming its lobe is missing or\n
faded the illusion is complete. There are several other examples however\n
and I believe EO is intended in each case=}\n
{.}\n
$/P13GM_*HIS\n

$cweartern/n<pr_QUARTERNE\n
{.\'}\n
{=Both Madden and B&L read QUARCERNE but Madden corrects the reading\n
to QUARTERNE in his errata. The head stroke of this scribe\'s C normally\n
points diagonally down and is not horizontal like this littera. It is\n
true that most of the scribe\'s Ts have a head stroke beinning to the\n
left of the upright, but not all do, especially after Carlingian\n
minuscule R where the head stroke would foul the shoulder of the R.\n
Words with RT for comparison are surprisingly rare apart from HEORTE\n
where the R is invariably 2-shaped after O. But cf. aeRT fol. 6va\n
line 3=}\n

{=sic - this is a supposed error for WIzELEN "wiles, tricks" (so Madden,\n
and MED. It does seem the simplest interpretation and is semantically\n
plausible. Otho does not have this line so is no help. I find the\n
addition of ED to the word an implausible scribal error, whatever the\n
poet may originally have intended. The word has the shape of a compound\n
noun and it is tempting to read it as a reflex of OE wi:gle:oY - "battle\n
song" or "war cry". Formally, however this is difficult, because in this\n
scribe\'s language reflexes of OE [e:o] are always spelled EO not E while\n
elsewhere he has WI-EAX and WIaeX "battle-axe" with no sign of yogh=}\n
